{"uri": "8235052983", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "13:56:06", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T13:56:06Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T13:55:25Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9098039269447327, "url": "https://cyprus-mail.com/2024/07/20/six-times-world-champion-reardon-dies-at-91/", "title": "Six-times world champion Reardon dies at 91", "body": "Six-times world snooker champion Ray Reardon has died at the age of 91 after being diagnosed with cancer, World Snooker Tour reported on Saturday.\n\nWelshman Reardon, who died late on Friday, dominated snooker in the 1970s, winning his first world title in 1970 and five more during the rest of the decade.\n\nNicknamed 'Dracula' due to his widow's peak hairstyle, Reardon was one of the most popular and charismatic figures in the sport. He continued to play snooker in his spare time after retiring in 1991 at the age of 58.\n\nThe winner's trophy of the Welsh Open was named the \"Ray Reardon Trophy\" in his honour in 2016. Reardon was also a mentor to Ronnie O'Sullivan, guiding the seven-times world champion to his second title in 2004.\n\n\"Ray is one of the best sports people ever from Wales and the best snooker player,\" Reardon's compatriot and three-times world champion Mark Williams said in a statement.\n\n\"He's one of the reasons why a lot of us started playing. He put snooker on the map, alongside Alex Higgins, Jimmy White and Steve Davis. Anyone playing now owes them a lot because they brought popularity to the game. He is a real inspiration.\"\n\nReardon beat his contemporary White in the 1983 International Masters to win his last title. \"Gutted to hear my very good friend Ray Reardon has passed away,\" White wrote in a post on X.\n\n\"A total class act and very kind to me when I was making my way in the game. A giant of the game. Rest in Peace mate.\"\n\nFour-times world champion Mark Selby wrote: \"Did not manage to be in his presence much but what a legend of our sport. He was one of the sport's most popular and charismatic players, known as 'Dracula' due to his distinctive hairstyle.\n\nThe Welshman was still playing snooker in recent months and made headlines in December after becoming one of the oldest players to make a century break. He was awarded an MBE 1985, was inducted into the snooker Hall of Fame in 2011 and his name will live on as the winner of the Welsh Open is presented with the Ray Reardon Trophy.\n\nFormer player and BBC commentator John Virgo tweeted: \"Sad news. Ray Reardon passed away last night, it was a honour to have known him, a true great of our game RIP.\"\n\nLegendary player Jimmy White tweeted: \"Gutted to hear my very good friend Ray Reardon has passed away. A total class act and very kind to me when I was making my way in the game. A giant of the game. Rest in Peace mate.\"\n\nSnooker star Mark Williams said: \"Ray is one of the best sports people ever from Wales and the best snooker player. He's one of the reasons why a lot of us started playing. He put snooker on the map, alongside Alex Higgins, Jimmy White and Steve Davis. Anyone playing now owes them a lot because they brought popularity to the game. He is a real inspiration.\"\n\nSpeaking at the start of 2023, ahead of the Masters Snooker event, Ronnie O'Sullivan explained the enduring legacy of Reardon. \"Ray Reardon brought a side to the game I never knew existed,\" O'Sullivan told Eurosport. \"I learnt it and now I appreciate it and use it a lot, and when I have to.\"\n\nReardon's victory over Perrie Mans in the 1978 World Championship final made him the oldest world champion at age 45. It was a record he held until a 46-year-old O'Sullivan claimed the prize for the seventh time in 2022.\n\nAfter winning six titles, the Welshman reached one more Crucible final in 1982 when, age 49, he was beaten by Alex Higgins. Of the modern era, only O'Sullivan and Stephen Henry, with seven, have won more World Championship titles than Reardon, who is level with Steve Davis on six.\n\nHe was born in 1932 in Tredegar and began playing snooker from a young age while also working in the mines. After moving to Stoke-on-Trent, Reardon became a police officer, while continuing to develop a reputation as one of the best amateur players in the UK.\n\nReardon decided to quit policing and turn professional in snooker in 1967, at the age of 35. {"uri": "8234939070", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "12:08:37", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T12:08:37Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T12:07:22Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8666666746139526, "url": "https://www.rte.ie/sport/snooker/2024/0720/1460948-snooker-great-ray-reardon-dies-aged-91/", "title": "Snooker great Ray Reardon dies aged 91", "body": "Six-time world snooker champion Ray Reardon has died at the age of 91.\n\nWelshman Reardon, who dominated the sport in the 1970s, died on Friday night, his wife Carol confirmed to the World Snooker Tour.\n\nA statement on the WST's official website said: \"Ray Reardon, widely regarded as one of the greatest snooker players ever and a six-time world champion, has died at the age of 91.\"\n\nReardon ruled snooker in the 1970s when the man known fondly as 'Dracula' became a household name.\n\nYet his story almost ended long before the Welshman with the widow's peak found stardom.\n\nOn 30 April, 1957, the world collapsed around the ears of the 24-year-old Reardon as a mining accident almost claimed his life.\n\nHe was fortunate to see the next day, let alone land six World Championship titles - his last in 1978 aged 45.\n\nReardon, who has died at the age of 91, was born on 8 October, 1932 in Tredegar.\n\nHe left Georgetown Secondary School at 14 and, after a brief stint as an apprentice motor mechanic, he followed his father Ben down the pits, firstly at nearby Ty Trist and later at the Pochin Colliery.\n\nThe teenage Reardon displayed an aptitude for snooker and was spared evening shifts to allow him to practise. Unlike in the modern game, however, there was little money to be made from potting balls.\n\nCutting coal underground was the career choice, and when Welsh mines began to close the Reardon family uprooted to North Staffordshire. Ben and Ray Reardon found work at the Florence Colliery in 1956 and in the following year disaster almost struck.\n\nThe future world-beating cueman was developing a pit roadway when a \"trickle of dirt\" fell on his helmet. Sensing danger, he hurled himself down and when a 12-foot girder crashed down along with a deluge of rubble, Reardon was trapped.\n\nHe was rescued after three hours, Reardon drawing on memories of playing marbles with brother Ron - 17 years his junior - to avoid panicking.\n\nIn his 1982 autobiography, Reardon recalled: \"My ordeal had left me looking and feeling a bit like a dirty dishcloth and when the blood started to circulate in my numb legs it had me screaming in pain for a moment or so, but they gave me sips of water and soon I was all right.\"\n\nDuring war time, mother Cynthia had bought the young Ray a 4ft x 2ft billiard table for Christmas and he exhibited natural talent.\n\nOne afternoon, after attending a headmaster's funeral, Reardon ducked out of a school line and into the Tredegar Workmen's Institute Society, where he won a frame against venue manager Tommy Biggs, who had a wooden leg. Reardon was swiftly collared by his maths teacher.\n\nFrom a young age he played cue sports for money. His father frowned on this, until learning his son was winning.\n\nBy the age of 13 Reardon was representing the Institute's men's team in the Sirhowy Valley Billiards and Snooker League. He was soon champion of all Wales.\n\nAs a miner, Reardon wore white gloves similar to those of a snooker referee, but after meeting future wife Sue his career moved above ground.\n\nRay and Sue married on Easter Monday in 1959 and Reardon joined the Stoke-on-Trent police force a year later, staying for almost eight years.\n\nHe won the English amateur championship in 1964, beating John Spencer, and a year later became a father when Sue gave birth to their son Darren. Daughter Melanie followed in 1969.\n\nReardon considered staying with the police, but snooker won out. The BBC television show Pot Black was a hit, even while many watched in black and white, and Reardon defeated Spencer to win the first edition in 1969.\n\nHe lost his opener at the World Championship in the same year 25-24 to the 55-year-old Fred Davis. But a year later, the 37-year-old Reardon ruled the snooker world for the first time, beating John Pulman 37-33 in the final. He said that \"nothing will ever surpass the wonder\" of the moment.\n\nReardon's first car was a Ford Prefect he labelled the 'Gutless Wonder'. Later, after upgrading, he owned the 1 PRO licence plate.\n\nManchester staged the 1974 World Championship and, come the closing session of the final against Graham Miles, Reardon introduced the outfit that would forever associate him with Bram Stoker's blood-sucking Count Dracula.\n\nStoke-based Marsden's Tailors sponsored Reardon and he emerged attired in cape, top hat and scarlet silk jacket.\n\n\"Someone decided the outfit made me look like Dracula and that name stuck to me more than most,\" Reardon later said.\n\nReardon for many years played exhibitions in South Africa during the apartheid era, explaining the trips by saying: \"The only world I was trying to change was the world of snooker.\"\n\nHe won four straight world titles from 1973 to 1976. Then, as defending champion, Reardon had the honour of playing on the first morning when the tournament moved to Sheffield's Crucible Theatre in 1977. He beat Patsy Fagan before falling to eventual champion Spencer.\n\nTwelve months later and Reardon reigned at the Crucible when a 25-18 victory over Perrie Mans clinched a sixth world title at the age of 45 years and 203 days.\n\nFour years later he returned to the final but was beaten 18-15 by Alex Higgins.\n\nReardon remained competitive until the mid-1980s and retired in 1991.\n\nHis marriage to Sue ended in divorce during the twilight years of his playing career.\n\nReardon made Brixham, and later Torquay, his home, and spent many happy years as president of Churston Golf Club.\n\nIn 2004 he teamed up with Ronnie O'Sullivan in a mentoring role at the World Championship and helped to steer the Rocket to his second world title. He had received a call from O'Sullivan's imprisoned father, Ronnie snr, to encourage the union.\n\nReardon, who is survived by his second wife Carol, saw the Welsh Open trophy named in his honour in 2016 but savoured his quiet retirement in Devon, saying: \"It's nice on the golf course.\"", "source": {"uri": "rte.ie", "dataType": "news", "title": "RTE.ie"}, "authors": [], "concepts": [{"uri": {"uri": "8234955055", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "12:23:39", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T12:23:39Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T12:22:08Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8666666746139526, "url": "https://www.con-telegraph.ie/2024/07/20/snooker-mourns-six-time-world-champion-ray-reardon-after-his-death-aged-91/", "title": "Snooker mourns six-time world champion Ray Reardon after his death, aged 91 | Connaught Telegraph", "body": "Snooker mourns six-time world champion Ray Reardon after his death, aged 91 RSS Syndicated Content Published: Sat 20 Jul 2024, 12:58 PM Last updated: Sat 20 Jul 2024, 1:18 PM\n\nPA Sport Staff\n\nSix-time world snooker champion Ray Reardon has died at the age of 91.\n\nThe Welshman died on Friday night, his wife Carol confirmed to the World Snooker Tour.\n\nA statement on the WST's official website said: \"Ray Reardon, widely regarded as one of the greatest snooker players ever and a six-time world champion, has died at the age of 91.\"\n\nReardon ruled the sport in the 1970s, when the man known fondly as 'Dracula' became a household name.\n\nYet his story almost ended long before the Welshman with the widow's peak found stardom.\n\nOn April 30th, 1957, the world collapsed around the ears of the 24-year-old Reardon as a mining accident almost claimed his life.\n\nHe was fortunate to see the next day, let alone land six World Championship titles - his last in 1978 aged 45.\n\nReardon was born on October 8th, 1932 in Tredegar, South Wales.\n\nHe left Georgetown Secondary School at 14 and, after a brief stint as an apprentice motor mechanic, he followed his father Ben down the pits, firstly at nearby Ty Trist and later at the Pochin Colliery.\n\nThe teenage Reardon displayed an aptitude for snooker and was spared evening shifts to allow him to practise. Unlike in the modern game, however, there was little money to be made from potting balls.\n\nCutting coal underground was the career choice, and when Welsh mines began to close the Reardon family uprooted to North Staffordshire. Ben and Ray Reardon found work at the Florence Colliery in 1956 and in the following year disaster almost struck.\n\nThe future world-beating cueman was developing a pit roadway when a \"trickle of dirt\" fell on his helmet. Sensing danger, he hurled himself down and when a 12-foot girder crashed down along with a deluge of rubble, Reardon was trapped.\n\nHe was rescued after three hours, Reardon drawing on memories of playing marbles with brother Ron - 17 years his junior - to avoid panicking.\n\nIn his 1982 autobiography, Reardon recalled: \"My ordeal had left me looking and feeling a bit like a dirty dishcloth and when the blood started to circulate in my numb legs it had me screaming in pain for a moment or so, but they gave me sips of water and soon I was all right.\"\n\nDuring wartime, mother Cynthia had bought the young Ray a 4ft x 2ft billiard table for Christmas and he exhibited natural talent.\n\nOne afternoon, after attending a headmaster's funeral, Reardon ducked out of a school line and into the Tredegar Workmen's Institute Society, where he won a frame against venue manager Tommy Biggs, who had a wooden leg. Reardon was swiftly collared by his maths teacher.\n\nFrom a young age, he played cue sports for money. His father frowned on this, until learning his son was winning.\n\nBy the age of 13, Reardon was representing the Institute's men's team in the Sirhowy Valley Billiards and Snooker League. He was soon champion of all Wales.\n\nAs a miner, Reardon wore white gloves similar to those of a snooker referee, but after meeting future wife Sue his career moved above ground.\n\nRay and Sue married on Easter Monday in 1959 and Reardon joined the Stoke-on-Trent police force a year later, staying for almost eight years.\n\nHe won the English amateur championship in 1964, beating John Spencer, and a year later became a father when Sue gave birth to their son Darren. Daughter Melanie followed in 1969.\n\nReardon considered staying with the police, but snooker won out. The BBC television show Pot Black was a hit, even while many watched in black and white, and Reardon defeated Spencer to win the first edition in 1969.\n\nHe lost his opener at the World Championship in the same year 25-24 to the 55-year-old Fred Davis. But a year later, the 37-year-old Reardon ruled the snooker world for the first time, beating John Pulman 37-33 in the final. He said that \"nothing will ever surpass the wonder\" of the moment.\n\nReardon's first car was a Ford Prefect he labelled the 'Gutless Wonder'. Later, after upgrading, he owned the 1 PRO licence plate.\n\nManchester staged the 1974 World Championship and, come the closing session of the final against Graham Miles, Reardon introduced the outfit that would forever associate him with Bram Stoker's blood-sucking Count Dracula.\n\nStoke-based Marsden's Tailors sponsored Reardon and he emerged attired in cape, top hat and scarlet silk jacket.\n\n\"Someone decided the outfit made me look like Dracula and that name stuck to me more than most,\" Reardon later said.\n\nReardon for many years played exhibitions in South Africa during the apartheid era, explaining the trips by saying: \"The only world I was trying to change was the world of snooker.\"\n\nHe won four straight world titles from 1973 to 1976. Then, as defending champion, Reardon had the honour of playing on the first morning when the tournament moved to Sheffield's Crucible Theatre in 1977. He beat Patsy Fagan before falling to eventual champion Spencer.\n\nTwelve months later, Reardon reigned at the Crucible when a 25-18 victory over Perrie Mans clinched a sixth world title at the age of 45 years and 203 days.\n\nFour years later, he returned to the final but was beaten 18-15 by Alex Higgins.\n\nReardon remained competitive until the mid-1980s and retired in 1991.\n\nHis marriage to Sue ended in divorce during the twilight years of his playing career.\n\nReardon made Brixham, and later Torquay, his home, and spent many happy years as president of Churston Golf Club.\n\nIn 2004, he teamed up with Ronnie O'Sullivan in a mentoring role at the World Championship and helped to steer the Rocket to his second world title. He had received a call from O'Sullivan's imprisoned father, Ronnie snr, to encourage the union.\n\nReardon, who is survived by his second wife Carol, saw the Welsh Open trophy named in his honour in 2016 but savoured his quiet retirement in Devon, saying: \"It's nice on the golf course.\"\n\nRSS Syndicated Content Published: Sat 20 Jul 2024, 12:58 PM Last updated: Sat 20 Jul 2024, 1:18 PM", contests on television at the time.\n\nReardon's first world title came in 1970 when he beat John Pulman by 39 frames to 34.\n\nThere followed a gap until 1973 when he beat Australian Eddie Charlton in the final, the first of four consecutive titles, culminating in his victory over Higgins in 1976 - the last Championship before the move to The Crucible in Sheffield.\n\nReardon won the title again in 1978, and topped the world rankings until 1981 when the emergence of Steve Davis heralded a new era.\n\nThe former coal miner and a police officer reached the World Championship final again in 1982, but lost to Higgins.\n\nReardon retired from the professional game in 1991 at the age of 58.\n\nHe was later hired by Ronnie O'Sullivan as a consultant and was credited with helping the talented A giant of the game. Rest in Peace mate.\"\n\nReardon won six world titles between 1970 and 1978, earning an MBE in the 1985 Queen's Birthday honours.\n\nNicknamed 'Dracula' after playing a session of the 1974 World Championship final in a cape, top hat and scarlet silk jacket, Reardon rose to prominence as snooker became a popular sport in Britain thanks to the Pot Black television programme.\n\n\"Someone decided the outfit made me look like Dracula and that name stuck to me more than most,\" Reardon said.\n\nHe battled the flamboyant Alex Higgins in a long-running rivalry with the \"Hurricane\" that included an epic 18-15 defeat in the 1982 World Championship final.\n\nThe former world number one worked as a coal miner and was also a police officer for eight years before taking snooker by storm.\n\nHe was fortunate to survive a mining accident when a 12-foot girder and a deluge of rubble crashed down on him while he was building a pit lane, leaving him trapped for three hours.\n\n\"My ordeal had left me looking and feeling a bit like a dirty dishcloth and when the blood started to circulate in my numb legs it had me screaming in pain for a moment or so, but they gave me sips of water and soon I was all right,\" he wrote in his autobiography.\n\nReardon retired in 1991 at the age of 58 but remained in the snooker world, helping Ronnie O'Sullivan as a consultant during his run to the 2004 world title.\n\nThe Welsh Open trophy was named in Reardon's honour in 2016. He passed away on Friday night after a battle with cancer.\n\nNicknamed 'Dracula', he became a household name as he dominated the World Championship in the 1970s.\n\nLeading the tributes, three-time Crucible king Mark Williams said: \"Ray is one of the best sports people ever from Wales and the best snooker player. He is a real inspiration.\"\n\nJimmy White wrote on social media: \"Gutted to hear my very good friend Ray Reardon has passed away.\n\n\"A total class act and very kind to me when I was making my way in the game. A giant of the game. Rest in Peace mate.\"\n\nReardon was still playing snooker in recent months, and remarkably made a century break last November, a few weeks after his 91st birthday.\n\nReardon won his first world championship in 1970 when he defeated John Pulman 37-33 in the final.\n\nThat was the beginning of his era of domination, as he went on to claim world titles in 1973, 1974, 1975, 1976 and 1978.\n\nIn that final victory in 1978 he became the oldest world champion at the age of 45, a record that was only surpassed by a 46-year-old Ronnie O'Sullivan in 2022.\n\nIt was also Reardon's only success at the Crucible, where the championship had moved in 1977.\n\nHe beat Perrie Mans 25-18 in the final and his modern-day record of six titles was not matched until Steve Davis reached that tally in 1989.\n\nReardon reached one more Crucible final in 1982 when he was beaten by Alex Higgins.\n\nIn 2004 he teamed up with O'Sullivan in a mentoring role at the World Championship and helped to steer the 'Rocket' to his second world title.\n\nReardon was awarded an MBE and was inducted into the snooker Hall of Fame in 2011.\n\nEach year the winner of the Welsh Open is presented with the Ray Rest in peace.\"\n\nFour-time world champion Mark Selby wrote: \"Terribly sad news to hear the passing of Ray Reardon. Did not manage to be in his presence much, but what a legend of our sport. RIP Ray.\"\n\nReardon was awarded the MBE 1985. He was inducted into the snooker Hall of Fame in 2011, and each year the winner of the Welsh Open is presented with the Ray Reardon Trophy. Throughout the snooker family, he was much loved and highly respected.\n\nThe father of two, who married his second wife Carol in 1987, lived for over 40 years in Devon, an area he had got to know through playing exhibitions.\n\nAfter retirement he continued to play snooker socially, as well as golf, becoming President of Churston Golf Club. {"uri": "8234929566", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "11:59:24", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T11:59:24Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T11:57:49Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8509804010391235, "url": "https://ca.news.yahoo.com/ray-reardon-six-time-world-115331301.html", "title": "Ray Reardon, six-time world snooker champion nicknamed 'Dracula', dies aged 91", "body": "Ray Reardon, the six-time world snooker champion who was affectionately nicknamed 'Dracula' for his widow's peak hairline and prominent teeth, has died at the age of 91 after battling cancer, his wife Carol confirmed.\n\nReardon - who was born in Tredegar, south Wales - dominated snooker in the 1970s as he won all six of his world titles in a nine-year spell, including four straight crowns from 1973-76.\n\nHis final World Snooker Championship win came in 1978, just a year after the event first moved to the iconic Crucible Theatre in Sheffield, at the age of 45 years and 203 days - making him the oldest world champion in history at the time. That record was only broken by Ronnie O'Sullivan, a player that Reardon had coached at one point, in 2022.\n\nLeading the tributes, three-time world champion Mark Williams said: \"Ray is one of the best sports people ever from Wales and the best snooker player.\n\n\"He's one of the reasons why a lot of us started playing. He put snooker on the map, alongside Alex Higgins, Jimmy White and Steve Davis. Anyone playing now owes them a lot because they brought popularity to the game. He is a real inspiration.\"\n\nBy the time of snooker's boom in the mid-1980s, when the sport exploded in popularity, the Welshman was a respected elder statesman of the game. He lost to long-time rival Alex 'Hurricane' Higgins in the memorable 1982 world final at the Crucible but, that same year, became the oldest player to win a ranking event by triumphing over Jimmy White in the final of the Professional Players Tournament at the age of 50 - a record that stands until this day.\n\nHe won two non-ranking tournaments, the Welsh Professional Championship and International Masters, in 1983 and eventually retired from professional competition in 1991. He never made a maximum 147 break in tournament play but did make the even-rarer 146.\n\nReardon won the inaugural Pot Black tournament in 1969 as snooker burst on to colour televisions across the UK and he was the first ever world No 1 when the rankings were introduced during the 1976-77 season, a position he held for the next five years.\n\nHe was appointed a Member of the Order of the British Empire (MBE) in 1985 for services to snooker and the Welsh Open trophy was renamed the Ray Reardon Trophy in his honour from the 2017 edition of the tournament onwards.\n\nHe lived in Devon in his later life and was president of the golf club in Churston, while managing to hit a century break in his local club at the age of 91.\n\nHis impact on the sport can also be measured by those who took inspiration from him. Steve Davis, the undisputed dominant force of the 1980s, had watched Reardon play at Pontins in 1975 and incorporated aspects of the then-43-year-old's game into his own. That included a short pause before hitting the cue ball, his overall approach to the shot and mimicking the way Reardon acted around the table, thinking he was special.\n\nAhead of the 2004 World Championship, Reardon mentored O'Sullivan by giving him psychological and tactical help, mostly focusing on his safety play. 'The Rocket' would go on to win his second world title by beating Graeme Dott 18-8 in the final that year and credited Reardon in his winner's speech. The two maintained a warm relationship for the remainder of his life. \"Ray Reardon brought a side to the game I never knew existed,\" O'Sullivan would later say, while citing Reardon as one of the three most influential people in his career. \"I learnt it and now I appreciate it and use it a lot.\"\n\nReardon, born in 1932 in the coal-mining community of Tredegar in Monmouthshire, had been introduced to cue sports as an eight-year-old by his uncle and although initially focused on English billiards, his aptitude for snooker became apparent. He won the News of the World amateur title in 1949 and had a successful amateur career for many years.\n\nHe initially worked as a miner at Ty Trist Colliery, wearing white gloves while mining to protect his hands for snooker but, after being buried in a rockfall for three hours in 1960, changed career to become a policeman and moved to Stoke in England. He would turn professional in 1967, at the age of 34, and his 1969 victory in the inaugural Pot Black tournament - a series of one-frame matches broadcast by the BBC - thrust him into prominence.\n\nHe won his first of six world titles in 1970 by defeating John Pulman 37-33 in the final and began a decade of snooker dominance, which included a satisfying 27-16 win over Higgins in the 1976 final. The gentlemanly, polite and steady Reardon stood in notable contrast to the wild, untamed, unreliable style of Higgins both on and off the table, adding a fascinating personal twist to their long-time rivalry. Higgins would earn his revenge in the 1982 world final leading to one of the most iconic moments in the history of snooker when the Northern Irishman beckoned his partner and baby daughter to come and celebrate lifting the trophy alongside him.\n\nReardon made a good living on the lucrative snooker exhibition circuit throughout his life and was a mainstay on British television screens throughout the 1970s and 80s with appearances on shows such as This is Your Life, Parkinson, A Question of Sport and The Paul Daniels Magic Show. It was on the latter where he first earned the nickname 'Dracula', with the mischievous Daniels suggesting the title due to the Weslhman's dark widow's peak hairline and sharp-toothed grin. The genial Reardon leaned into the nickname which stuck with him for the rest of his career.\n\nHe married Sue, a pottery painter, in 1959 and the pair had two children together before divorcing in 1986. Reardon had left his wife in 1985 to live with Carol Covington and he told Daily Mirror reporters that that Sue had been \"fully informed\" of his eight-year affair with Covington, who he would go on More info\n\nSnooker titan Ray Reardon has died at the age of 91.\n\nReardon dominated the snooker scene in the 1970s, bagging himself the World Snooker Championship title six times - in 1970, 1973, 1974, 1975, 1976 and 1978. He's remembered not just for his game but also his playful 'Dracula' persona, which stemmed from his unique hairstyle.\n\nDespite his age, the Welsh snooker master was still active in the sport, even making headlines recently when he made a century break, becoming one of the oldest players to pull it off. His legacy includes an MBE awarded in 1985, induction into the snooker Hall of Fame in 2011 and the presentation of the Welsh Open's Ray Reardon Trophy to its winner.\n\nJohn Virgo, former player and BBC pundit, took to Twitter to share his feelings, posting: \"Sad news. Ray Reardon passed away last night, it was a honour to have known him, a true great of our game RIP.\"\n\nJimmy White, a fellow snooker legend, also tweeted to share his loss: \"Gutted to hear my very good friend Ray Reardon has passed away. A total class act and very kind to me when I was making my way in the game. A giant of the game. Rest in Peace mate,\" reports the Mirror.\n\nMark Williams, another star of the snooker world, paid tribute: \"Ray is one of the best sports people ever from Wales and the best snooker player. He's one of the reasons why a lot of us started playing. He put snooker on the map, alongside Alex Higgins, Jimmy White and Steve Davis. Anyone playing now owes them a lot because they brought popularity to the game. He is a real inspiration.\"\n\nAs we usher in 2023 ahead of the iconic Masters Snooker event, Ronnie O'Sullivan has taken time to reflect on the lasting impact of the legendary Ray Reardon. As O'Sullivan paints with admiration, \"Ray Reardon brought a side to the game I never knew existed,\" in a heartwarming tribute he shared with Eurosport.\n\n\"I learnt it and now I appreciate it and use it a lot, and when I have to.\"\n\nThe historical significance of Reardon's triumphant victory against Perrie Mans in the final round of the 1978 World Championship is carved in gold, making him the oldest world champion at the age of 45. This record stood victorious until 46 year old O'Sullivan surpassed this benchmark by clinching the esteemed prize for the seventh time in 2022.\n\nAfter his dazzling journey of securing six titles, the Welshman ventured into more championship territory by reaching the extent of the Crucible final in 1982 when he was 49. He displayed tremendous perseverance against Alex Higgins.\n\nTo this day, only O'Sullivan and Stephen Henry can boast of emulating his record of seven victories. Remarkably, Reardon stands shoulder to shoulder with Steve Davis totalling six glorious World Championship titles.\n\nReardon's story begins in 1932, in the quaint town of Tredegar. Born amid modest beginnings, his life took an interesting turn when he started playing snooker casually while juggling his job in the mines.\n\nHis innate talent didn't go unnoticed when after moving to Stoke-on-Trent, he became a police officer. Even then, he continued to nurture his love for snooker, building his reputation as one of the most skilled amateur players in the UK.\n\nIn a twist that would change his fate forever, Reardon decided to hang up his police boots and focused his career solely on professional snooker at the age of 35 in 1967. It was a decision that quickly bore fruit as he showed his true mettle by ruling the snooker spectrum throughout the 1970s.\n\nDon't miss the latest news from around Scotland and beyond - Sign up to {"uri": "8234937380", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "12:09:22", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T12:09:22Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T12:05:37Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.843137264251709, "url": "https://www.dailymail.co.uk/wires/pa/article-13654241/Ray-Reardon-The-world-beating-cueman-swapped-coal-Crucible.html", "title": "Ray Reardon: The world-beating cueman who swapped coal mine for...", "body": "Ray Reardon ruled snooker in the 1970s when the man known fondly as 'Dracula' became a household name.\n\nYet his story almost ended long before the Welshman with the widow's peak found stardom.\n\nOn April 30, 1957, the world collapsed around the ears of the 24-year-old Reardon as a mining accident almost claimed his life.\n\nHe was fortunate to see the next day, let alone land six World Championship titles - his last in 1978 aged 45.\n\nReardon, who has died at the age of 91, was born on October 8, 1932 in Tredegar.\n\nHe left Georgetown Secondary School at 14 and, after a brief stint as an apprentice motor mechanic, he followed his father Ben down the pits, firstly at nearby Ty Trist and later at the Pochin Colliery.\n\nThe teenage Reardon displayed an aptitude for snooker and was spared evening shifts to allow him to practise. Unlike in the modern game, however, there was little money to be made from potting balls.\n\nCutting coal underground was the career choice, and when Welsh mines began to close the Reardon family uprooted to North Staffordshire. Ben and Ray Reardon found work at the Florence Colliery in 1956 and in the following year disaster almost struck.\n\nThe future world-beating cueman was developing a pit roadway when a \"trickle of dirt\" fell on his helmet. Sensing danger, he hurled himself down and when a 12-foot girder crashed down along with a deluge of rubble, Reardon was trapped.\n\nHe was rescued after three hours, Reardon drawing on memories of playing marbles with brother Ron - 17 years his junior - to avoid panicking.\n\nIn his 1982 autobiography, Reardon recalled: \"My ordeal had left me looking and feeling a bit like a dirty dishcloth and when the blood started to circulate in my numb legs it had me screaming in pain for a moment or so, but they gave me sips of water and soon I was all right.\"\n\nDuring war time, mother Cynthia had bought the young Ray a 4ft x 2ft billiard table for Christmas and he exhibited natural talent.\n\nOne afternoon, after attending a headmaster's funeral, Reardon ducked out of a school line and into the Tredegar Workmen's Institute Society, where he won a frame against venue manager Tommy Biggs, who had a wooden leg. Reardon was swiftly collared by his maths teacher.\n\nRay Reardon beat South Africa\u00b4s Perrie Mans (left) to win the world title in 1978 (PA).\n\nFrom a young age he played cue sports for money. His father frowned on this, until learning his son was winning.\n\nBy the age of 13 Reardon was representing the Institute's men's team in the Sirhowy Valley Billiards and Snooker League. He was soon champion of all Wales.\n\nAs a miner, Reardon wore white gloves similar to those of a snooker referee, but after meeting future wife Sue his career moved above ground.\n\nRay and Sue married on Easter Monday in 1959 and Reardon joined the Stoke-on-Trent police force a year later, staying for almost eight years.\n\nHe won the English amateur championship in 1964, beating John Spencer, and a year later became a father when Sue gave birth to their son Darren. Daughter Melanie followed in 1969.\n\nReardon considered staying with the police, but snooker won out. The BBC television show Pot Black was a hit, even while many watched in black and white, and Reardon defeated Spencer to win the first edition in 1969.\n\nHe lost his opener at the World Championship in the same year 25-24 to the 55-year-old Fred Davis. But a year later, the 37-year-old Reardon ruled the snooker world for the first time, beating John Pulman 37-33 in the final. He said that \"nothing will ever surpass the wonder\" of the moment.\n\nReardon's first car was a Ford Prefect he labelled the 'Gutless Wonder'. Later, after upgrading, he owned the 1 PRO licence plate.\n\nManchester staged the 1974 World Championship and, come the closing session of the final against Graham Miles, Reardon introduced the outfit that would forever associate him with Bram Stoker's blood-sucking Count Dracula.\n\nStoke-based Marsden's Tailors sponsored Reardon and he emerged attired in cape, top hat and scarlet silk jacket.\n\n\"Someone decided the outfit made me look like Dracula and that name stuck to me more than most,\" Reardon later said.\n\nReardon for many years played exhibitions in South Africa during the apartheid era, explaining the trips by saying: \"The only world I was trying to change was the world of snooker.\"\n\nRay Reardon served as a mentor for Ronnie O\u00b4Sullivan at the 2004 World Championship (Gareth Copley/PA).\n\nHe won four straight world titles from 1973 to 1976. Then, as defending champion, Reardon had the honour of playing on the first morning when the tournament moved to Sheffield's Crucible Theatre in 1977. He beat Patsy Fagan before falling to eventual champion Spencer.\n\nTwelve months later and Reardon reigned at the Crucible when a 25-18 victory over Perrie Mans clinched a sixth world title at the age of 45 years and 203 days.\n\nFour years later he returned to the final but was beaten 18-15 by Alex Higgins.\n\nReardon remained competitive until the mid-1980s and retired in 1991.\n\nHis marriage to Sue ended in divorce during the twilight years of his playing career.\n\nReardon made Brixham, and later Torquay, his home, and spent many happy years as president of Churston Golf Club.\n\nIn 2004 he teamed up with Ronnie O'Sullivan in a mentoring role at the World Championship and helped to steer the Rocket to his second world title. He had received a call from O'Sullivan's imprisoned father, Ronnie snr, to encourage the union.\n\nReardon, who is survived by his second wife Carol, saw the Welsh Open trophy named in his honour in 2016 but savoured his quiet retirement in Devon, saying: \"It's nice on the golf course.\"", "source": {"uri": "dailymail.co.uk", "dataType": "news", "title": "Daily Mail Online"}, "authors": [], {"uri": "2024-07-428062683", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "12:34:09", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T12:34:09Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T12:22:47Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8313725590705872, "url": "https://www.aol.co.uk/sport/snooker-mourns-ray-reardon-death-120143389.html", "title": "Snooker mourns Ray Reardon after his death at the age of 91", "body": "Snooker mourns Ray Reardon after his death at the age of 91\n\nDamian Spellman, PA\n\n20 July 2024 at 1:22 pm\n\nSnooker has lost one of its greats following the death of six-time world champion Ray Reardon at the age of 91.\n\nThe popular Welshman, nicknamed \"Dracula\" because of his distinctive widow's peak hairstyle, died on Friday night after a battle with cancer, his wife Carol has confirmed.\n\nA former coal miner and police officer, Reardon was at the forefront of the game's rise to popularity as a televised sport during the 1970s and is credited with influencing the next generation of superstars.\n\nCompatriot and three-time world champion Mark Williams told the World Snooker Tour's official website: \"Ray is one of the best sports people ever from Wales and the best snooker player. He's one of the reasons why a lot of us started playing.\n\n\"He put snooker on the map, alongside Alex Higgins, Jimmy White and Steve Davis. Anyone playing now owes them a lot because they brought popularity to the game. He is a real inspiration.\"\n\nWhite paid his own tribute in a post on X which read: \"Gutted to hear my very good friend Ray Reardon has passed away. A total class act and very kind to me when I was making my way in the game. A giant of the game. Rest in Peace, mate.\"\n\nBorn in Tredegar in 1932, Reardon took up snooker and billiards as a youngster and, having followed his father down the pit at Ty Trist Colliery, proved so adept at the former that he won the Welsh Amateur Championship every year from 1950 to 1955.\n\nHe swapped the mines for a career with the police but at the age of 35, and after beating John Spencer to lift the English Amateur title in 1964, he turned professional.\n\nReardon's first world championship campaign ended in a 25-24 quarter-final defeat by Fred Davis in 1969, but he won the title for the first time a year later and repeated the feat in 1973, 1974, 1975, 1976 and 1978, the last of his wins coming at the Crucible after the tournament's move to the Sheffield venue 12 months earlier.\n\nThat win over South Africa's Perrie Mans established the then 45-year-old as the oldest man to claim the world crown, a record surpassed by Ronnie O'Sullivan in 2022.\n\nReardon reached one more world final in 1982, when he was beaten by Higgins, but won 16 other professional tournaments during a career which saw him crowned as the sport's first number one when rankings were introduced in 1975.\n\nHe retired in 1991, but continued to play into his nineties and compiled a century break in November last year.\n\nReardon's death prompted an outpouring of affection for a man whose warmth and humour helped to make him a household name.\n\nShaun Murphy, world champion in 2005, said on X: \"What a man you were Ray. An absolute animal on the table, and a total gentleman off it. It was an honour to spend time with you whether we played snooker or golf - I'll treasure every minute. Rest in peace.\"\n\nFormer player and current TV presenter Neal Foulds posted: \"Awfully sad news that Ray Reardon has passed away. A giant of our sport. Thanks for the memories Ray. Rest in peace.\"\n\nTV personality Piers Morgan said: \"Sad news. Brilliant player, and such a classy gentleman. Unlike in the modern game, however, there was little money to be made from potting balls.\n\nCutting coal underground was the career choice, and when Welsh mines began to close the Reardon family uprooted to North Staffordshire. Ben and Ray Reardon found work at the Florence Colliery in 1956 and in the following year disaster almost struck.\n\nThe future world-beating cueman was developing a pit roadway when a \"trickle of dirt\" fell on his helmet. Sensing danger, he hurled himself down and when a 12-foot girder crashed down along with a deluge of rubble, Reardon was trapped.\n\nHe was rescued after three hours, Reardon drawing on memories of playing marbles with brother Ron - 17 years his junior - to avoid panicking.\n\nIn his 1982 autobiography, Reardon recalled: \"My ordeal had left me looking and feeling a bit like a dirty dishcloth and when the blood started to circulate in my numb legs it had me screaming in pain for a moment or so, but they gave me sips of water and soon I was all right.\"\n\nDuring war time, mother Cynthia had bought the young Ray a 4ft x 2ft billiard table for Christmas and he exhibited natural talent.\n\nOne afternoon, after attending a headmaster's funeral, Reardon ducked out of a school line and into the Tredegar Workmen's Institute Society, where he won a frame against venue manager Tommy Biggs, who had a wooden leg. Reardon was swiftly collared by his maths teacher.\n\nFrom a young age he played cue sports for money. His father frowned on this, until learning his son was winning.\n\nBy the age of 13 Reardon was representing the Institute's men's team in the Sirhowy Valley Billiards and Snooker League. He was soon champion of all Wales.\n\nAs a miner, Reardon wore white gloves similar to those of a snooker referee, but after meeting future wife Sue his career moved above ground.\n\nRay and Sue married on Easter Monday in 1959 and Reardon joined the Stoke-on-Trent police force a year later, staying for almost eight years.\n\nHe won the English amateur championship in 1964, beating John Spencer, and a year later became a father when Sue gave birth to their son Darren. Daughter Melanie followed in 1969.\n\nReardon considered staying with the police, but snooker won out. The BBC television show Pot Black was a hit, even while many watched in black and white, and Reardon defeated Spencer to win the first edition in 1969.\n\nHe lost his opener at the World Championship in the same year 25-24 to the 55-year-old Fred Davis. But a year later, the 37-year-old Reardon ruled the snooker world for the first time, beating John Pulman 37-33 in the final. He said that \"nothing will ever surpass the wonder\" of the moment.\n\nReardon's first car was a Ford Prefect he labelled the 'Gutless Wonder'. Later, after upgrading, he owned the 1 PRO licence plate.\n\nManchester staged the 1974 World Championship and, come the closing session of the final against Graham Miles, Reardon introduced the outfit that would forever associate him with Bram Stoker's blood-sucking Count Dracula.\n\nStoke-based Marsden's Tailors sponsored Reardon and he emerged attired in cape, top hat and scarlet silk jacket.\n\n\"Someone decided the outfit made me look like Dracula and that name stuck to me more than most,\" Reardon later said.\n\nReardon for many years played exhibitions in South Africa during the apartheid era, explaining the trips by saying: \"The only world I was trying to change was the world of snooker.\"\n\nHe won four straight world titles from 1973 to 1976. Then, as defending champion, Reardon had the honour of playing on the first morning when the tournament moved to Sheffield's Crucible Theatre in 1977. He beat Patsy Fagan before falling to eventual champion Spencer.\n\nTwelve months later and Reardon reigned at the Crucible when a 25-18 victory over Perrie Mans clinched a sixth world title at the age of 45 years and 203 days.\n\nFour years later he returned to the final but was beaten 18-15 by Alex Higgins.\n\nReardon remained competitive until the mid-1980s and retired in 1991.\n\nHis marriage to Sue ended in divorce during the twilight years of his playing career.\n\nReardon made Brixham, and later Torquay, his home, and spent many happy years as president of Churston Golf Club.\n\nIn 2004 he teamed up with Ronnie O'Sullivan in a mentoring role at the World Championship and helped to steer the Rocket to his second world title. He had received a call from O'Sullivan's imprisoned father, Ronnie snr, to encourage the union.\n\nReardon, who is survived by his second wife Carol, saw the Welsh Open trophy named in his honour in 2016 but savoured his quiet retirement in Devon, saying: \"It's nice on the golf course.\"", "source": {"uri": "heraldscotland.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "The Herald"}, "authors": [], {"uri": "8234924932", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "11:53:51", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T11:53:51Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T11:52:39Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8274509906768799, "url": "https://www.independent.co.uk/sport/snooker/ray-reardon-death-cause-snooker-dracula-b2517315.html", "title": "Ray Reardon, six-time world snooker champion nicknamed 'Dracula', dies aged 91", "body": "Our mission is to deliver unbiased, fact-based reporting that holds power to account and exposes the truth.\n\nWhether $5 or $50, every contribution counts.\n\nSupport us to deliver journalism without an agenda.\n\nRay Reardon, the six-time world snooker champion who was affectionately nicknamed 'Dracula' for his widow's peak hairline and prominent teeth, has died at the age of 91 after battling cancer, his wife Carol confirmed.\n\nReardon - who was born in Tredegar, south Wales - dominated snooker in the 1970s as he won all six of his world titles in a nine-year spell, including four straight crowns from 1973-76.\n\nHis final World Snooker Championship win came in 1978, just a year after the event first moved to the iconic Crucible Theatre in Sheffield, at the age of 45 years and 203 days - making him the oldest world champion in history at the time. That record was only broken by Ronnie O'Sullivan, a player that Reardon had coached at one point, in 2022.\n\nLeading the tributes, three-time world champion Mark Williams said: \"Ray is one of the best sports people ever from Wales and the best snooker player.\n\n\"He's one of the reasons why a lot of us started playing. He put snooker on the map, alongside Alex Higgins, Jimmy White and Steve Davis. Anyone playing now owes them a lot because they brought popularity to the game. He is a real inspiration.\"\n\nBy the time of snooker's boom in the mid-1980s, when the sport exploded in popularity, the Welshman was a respected elder statesman of the game. He lost to long-time rival Alex 'Hurricane' Higgins in the memorable 1982 world final at the Crucible but, that same year, became the oldest player to win a ranking event by triumphing over Jimmy White in the final of the Professional Players Tournament at the age of 50 - a record that stands until this day.\n\nHe won two non-ranking tournaments, the Welsh Professional Championship and International Masters, in 1983 and eventually retired from professional competition in 1991. He never made a maximum 147 break in tournament play but did make the even-rarer 146.\n\nReardon won the inaugural Pot Black tournament in 1969 as snooker burst on to colour televisions across the UK and he was the first ever world No 1 when the rankings were introduced during the 1976-77 season, a position he held for the next five years.\n\nHe was appointed a Member of the Order of the British Empire (MBE) in 1985 for services to snooker and the Welsh Open trophy was renamed the Ray Reardon Trophy in his honour from the 2017 edition of the tournament onwards.\n\nHe lived in Devon in his later life and was president of the golf club in Churston, while managing to hit a century break in his local club at the age of 91.\n\nHis impact on the sport can also be measured by those who took inspiration from him. Steve Davis, the undisputed dominant force of the 1980s, had watched Reardon play at Pontins in 1975 and incorporated aspects of the then-43-year-old's game into his own. That included a short pause before hitting the cue ball, his overall approach to the shot and mimicking the way Reardon acted around the table, thinking he was special.\n\nAhead of the 2004 World Championship, Reardon mentored O'Sullivan by giving him psychological and tactical help, mostly focusing on his safety play. 'The Rocket' would go on to win his second world title by beating Graeme Dott 18-8 in the final that year and credited Reardon in his winner's speech. The two maintained a warm relationship for the remainder of his life. \"Ray Reardon brought a side to the game I never knew existed,\" O'Sullivan would later say, while citing Reardon as one of the three most influential people in his career. \"I learnt it and now I appreciate it and use it a lot.\"\n\nReardon, born in 1932 in the coal-mining community of Tredegar in Monmouthshire, had been introduced to cue sports as an eight-year-old by his uncle and although initially focused on English billiards, his aptitude for snooker became apparent. He won the News of the World amateur title in 1949 and had a successful amateur career for many years.\n\nHe initially worked as a miner at Ty Trist Colliery, wearing white gloves while mining to protect his hands for snooker but, after being buried in a rockfall for three hours in 1960, changed career to become a policeman and moved to Stoke in England. He would turn professional in 1967, at the age of 34, and his 1969 victory in the inaugural Pot Black tournament - a series of one-frame matches broadcast by the BBC - thrust him into prominence.\n\nHe won his first of six world titles in 1970 by defeating John Pulman 37-33 in the final and began a decade of snooker dominance, which included a satisfying 27-16 win over Higgins in the 1976 final. The gentlemanly, polite and steady Reardon stood in notable contrast to the wild, untamed, unreliable style of Higgins both on and off the table, adding a fascinating personal twist to their long-time rivalry. Higgins would earn his revenge in the 1982 world final leading to one of the most iconic moments in the history of snooker when the Northern Irishman beckoned his partner and baby daughter to come and celebrate lifting the trophy alongside him.\n\nReardon made a good living on the lucrative snooker exhibition circuit throughout his life and was a mainstay on British television screens throughout the 1970s and 80s with appearances on shows such as This is Your Life, Parkinson, A Question of Sport and The Paul Daniels Magic Show. It was on the latter where he first earned the nickname 'Dracula', with the mischievous Daniels suggesting the title due to the Weslhman's dark widow's peak hairline and sharp-toothed grin. The genial Reardon leaned into the nickname which stuck with him for the rest of his career.\n\nHe married Sue, a pottery painter, in 1959 and the pair had two children together before divorcing in 1986. Reardon had left his wife in 1985 to live with Carol Covington and he told Daily Mirror reporters that that Sue had been \"fully informed\" of his eight-year affair with Covington, who he would go on I have this ability to pot balls and this gift that I find all ball sports easy really but I still feel like I've needed help and guidance along the way to make me better, better and better. And once I'm shown what to do I can only incorporate that into my game.\n\n\"The word was 'I'm going to make you into an impregnable player' and I didn't even know what 'impregnable' meant. I thought, alright, well, if that's what you think I need then we'll go along those lines. And then it was just slowly after working in some practice sessions I thought we were just going to pot balls.\"\n\nReardon won five ranking events and was a pioneer of the game alongside the likes of Steve Davis and Alex Higgins.\n\nAmong the tributes was a message from former player and rival Jimmy White, which read: \"Gutted to hear my very good friend Ray Reardon has passed away. A total class act and very kind to me when I was making my way in the game. A giant of the game. Rest in Peace mate.\"\n\nHis countryman Mark Williams, a three-time world champion, said: \"Ray is one of the best sports people ever from Wales and the best snooker player.\n\n\"He's one of the reasons why a lot of us started playing. He put snooker on the map, alongside Alex Higgins, Jimmy White and Steve Davis. Anyone playing now owes them a lot because they brought popularity to the game. He is a real inspiration.\"\n\nAnother ex-player and BBC commentator John Virgo wrote: \"Sad news. {"uri": "2024-07-428032206", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "11:48:42", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T11:48:42Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T11:42:14Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8235294222831726, "url": "https://www.aol.co.uk/ray-reardon-world-beating-cueman-114214477.html", "title": "Ray Reardon: The world-beating cueman who swapped coal mine for Crucible", "body": "Ray Reardon: The world-beating cueman who swapped coal mine for Crucible\n\nPA Sport Staff\n\n20 July 2024 at 1:42 pm\n\nRay Reardon ruled snooker in the 1970s when the man known fondly as 'Dracula' became a household name.\n\nYet his story almost ended long before the Welshman with the widow's peak found stardom.\n\nOn April 30, 1957, the world collapsed around the ears of the 24-year-old Reardon as a mining accident almost claimed his life.\n\nHe was fortunate to see the next day, let alone land six World Championship titles - his last in 1978 aged 45.\n\nReardon, who has died at the age of 91, was born on October 8, 1932 in Tredegar.\n\nHe left Georgetown Secondary School at 14 and, after a brief stint as an apprentice motor mechanic, he followed his father Ben down the pits, firstly at nearby Ty Trist and later at the Pochin Colliery.\n\nThe teenage Reardon displayed an aptitude for snooker and was spared evening shifts to allow him to practise. Unlike in the modern game, however, there was little money to be made from potting balls.\n\nCutting coal underground was the career choice, and when Welsh mines began to close the Reardon family uprooted to North Staffordshire. Ben and Ray Reardon found work at the Florence Colliery in 1956 and in the following year disaster almost struck.\n\nThe future world-beating cueman was developing a pit roadway when a \"trickle of dirt\" fell on his helmet. Sensing danger, he hurled himself down and when a 12-foot girder crashed down along with a deluge of rubble, Reardon was trapped.\n\nHe was rescued after three hours, Reardon drawing on memories of playing marbles with brother Ron - 17 years his junior - to avoid panicking.\n\nIn his 1982 autobiography, Reardon recalled: \"My ordeal had left me looking and feeling a bit like a dirty dishcloth and when the blood started to circulate in my numb legs it had me screaming in pain for a moment or so, but they gave me sips of water and soon I was all right.\"\n\nDuring war time, mother Cynthia had bought the young Ray a 4ft x 2ft billiard table for Christmas and he exhibited natural talent.\n\nOne afternoon, after attending a headmaster's funeral, Reardon ducked out of a school line and into the Tredegar Workmen's Institute Society, where he won a frame against venue manager Tommy Biggs, who had a wooden leg. Reardon was swiftly collared by his maths teacher.\n\nFrom a young age he played cue sports for money. His father frowned on this, until learning his son was winning.\n\nBy the age of 13 Reardon was representing the Institute's men's team in the Sirhowy Valley Billiards and Snooker League. He was soon champion of all Wales.\n\nAs a miner, Reardon wore white gloves similar to those of a snooker referee, but after meeting future wife Sue his career moved above ground.\n\nRay and Sue married on Easter Monday in 1959 and Reardon joined the Stoke-on-Trent police force a year later, staying for almost eight years.\n\nHe won the English amateur championship in 1964, beating John Spencer, and a year later became a father when Sue gave birth to their son Darren. Daughter Melanie followed in 1969.\n\nReardon considered staying with the police, but snooker won out. The BBC television show Pot Black was a hit, even while many watched in black and white, and Reardon defeated Spencer to win the first edition in 1969.\n\nHe lost his opener at the World Championship in the same year 25-24 to the 55-year-old Fred Davis. But a year later, the 37-year-old Reardon ruled the snooker world for the first time, beating John Pulman 37-33 in the final. He said that \"nothing will ever surpass the wonder\" of the moment.\n\nReardon's first car was a Ford Prefect he labelled the 'Gutless Wonder'. Later, after upgrading, he owned the 1 PRO licence plate.\n\nManchester staged the 1974 World Championship and, come the closing session of the final against Graham Miles, Reardon introduced the outfit that would forever associate him with Bram Stoker's blood-sucking Count Dracula.\n\nStoke-based Marsden's Tailors sponsored Reardon and he emerged attired in cape, top hat and scarlet silk jacket.\n\n\"Someone decided the outfit made me look like Dracula and that name stuck to me more than most,\" Reardon later said.\n\nReardon for many years played exhibitions in South Africa during the apartheid era, explaining the trips by saying: \"The only world I was trying to change was the world of snooker.\"\n\nHe won four straight world titles from 1973 to 1976. Then, as defending champion, Reardon had the honour of playing on the first morning when the tournament moved to Sheffield's Crucible Theatre in 1977. He beat Patsy Fagan before falling to eventual champion Spencer.\n\nTwelve months later and Reardon reigned at the Crucible when a 25-18 victory over Perrie Mans clinched a sixth world title at the age of 45 years and 203 days.\n\nFour years later he returned to the final but was beaten 18-15 by Alex Higgins.\n\nReardon remained competitive until the mid-1980s and retired in 1991.\n\nHis marriage to Sue ended in divorce during the twilight years of his playing career.\n\nReardon made Brixham, and later Torquay, his home, and spent many happy years as president of Churston Golf Club.\n\nIn 2004 he teamed up with Ronnie O'Sullivan in a mentoring role at the World Championship and helped to steer the Rocket to his second world title. He had received a call from O'Sullivan's imprisoned father, Ronnie snr, to encourage the union.\n\nReardon, who is survived by his second wife Carol, saw the Welsh Open trophy named in his honour in 2016 but savoured his quiet retirement in Devon, saying: \"It's nice on the golf course.\"", "source": {"uri": "aol.co.uk", "dataType": "news", "title": "Yahoo News"}, "authors": [], "concepts": {"uri": "8234996710", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "13:03:01", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T13:03:01Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T13:02:01Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8117647171020508, "url": "https://www.impartialreporter.com/sport/national/24465498.snooker-mourns-ray-reardon-death-age-91/", "title": "Snooker mourns Ray Reardon after his death at the age of 91", "body": "Snooker has lost one of its greats following the death of six-time world champion Ray Reardon at the age of 91.\n\nThe popular Welshman, nicknamed \"Dracula\" because of his distinctive widow's peak hairstyle, died on Friday night after a battle with cancer, his wife Carol has confirmed.\n\nA former coal miner and police officer, Reardon was at the forefront of the game's rise to popularity as a televised sport during the 1970s and is credited with influencing the next generation of superstars.\n\nCompatriot and three-time world champion Mark Williams told the World Snooker Tour's official website: \"Ray is one of the best sports people ever from Wales and the best snooker player. He's one of the reasons why a lot of us started playing.\n\n\"He put snooker on the map, alongside Alex Higgins, Jimmy White and Steve Davis. Anyone playing now owes them a lot because they brought popularity to the game. He is a real inspiration.\"\n\nWhite paid his own tribute in a post on X which read: \"Gutted to hear my very good friend Ray Reardon has passed away. A total class act and very kind to me when I was making my way in the game. A giant of the game. Rest in Peace, mate.\"\n\nBorn in Tredegar in 1932, Reardon took up snooker and billiards as a youngster and, having followed his father down the pit at Ty Trist Colliery, proved so adept at the former that he won the Welsh Amateur Championship every year from 1950 to 1955.\n\nHe swapped the mines for a career with the police but at the age of 35, and after beating John Spencer to lift the English Amateur title in 1964, he turned professional.\n\nReardon's first world championship campaign ended in a 25-24 quarter-final defeat by Fred Davis in 1969, but he won the title for the first time a year later and repeated the feat in 1973, 1974, 1975, 1976 and 1978, the last of his wins coming at the Crucible after the tournament's move to the Sheffield venue 12 months earlier.\n\nThat win over South Africa's Perrie Mans established the then 45-year-old as the oldest man to claim the world crown, a record surpassed by Ronnie O'Sullivan in 2022.\n\nReardon reached one more world final in 1982, when he was beaten by Higgins, but won 16 other professional tournaments during a career which saw him crowned as the sport's first number one when rankings were introduced in 1975.\n\nHe retired in 1991, but continued to play into his nineties and compiled a century break in November last year.\n\nReardon's death prompted an outpouring of affection for a man whose warmth and humour helped to make him a household name.\n\nDennis Taylor, winner of the 1985 world championship, said on X: \"So sad to hear of the passing of the great Ray Reardon. Would always be in my top 8 of all time.\n\n\"Had the pleasure of traveling around the world with a true gentleman. Condolences to all his family. RIP Ray.\"\n\nShaun Murphy, world champion in 2005, said: \"What a man you were Ray. An absolute animal on the table, and a total gentleman off it. It was an honour to spend time with you whether we played snooker or golf - I'll treasure every minute. Rest in peace.\"\n\nFormer player and current TV presenter Neal Foulds posted: \"Awfully sad news that Ray Reardon has passed away. A giant of our sport. Thanks for the memories Ray. Rest in peace.\"\n\nTV personality Piers Morgan said: \"Sad news. Brilliant player, and such a classy gentleman. turning down a place at Grammar School to do so.\n\nHe had taken up snooker six years prior and played as an amateur alongside his work as a miner.\n\nAt the age of 28 Reardon quit mining to become a POLICEMAN after he nearly died after being buried under rubble for three hours following a rock fall.\n\nTen years later he won the World Snooker Championship for the first time and collected just \u00a31,225 in prize money (the winner this year received \u00a3500,000), before going on to dominate the sport throughout the 1970s.\n\nReardan added five more world titles to his collection - a record not broken until Stephen Hendry won his seventh tournament in 1999.\n\nThe Welshman held another record for decades - being the oldest player to win the World Snooker Championship after claiming victory at the Crucible aged 45.\n\nRonnie O'Sullivan broke that record in 2022 but possibly would not have done so if not for Reardon's help.\n\nThe snooker legend worked with O'Sullivan for two years and played a big role in his World Championship success in 2004.\n\nO'Sullivan credits Reardon for transforming his game, describing his time with the icon as the \"best two years\" of his life.\n\nThe Rocket said of Reardon's tutelage: \"I knew I needed something you know and I kind of felt my game was good but I was always thirsty to see if somebody could make me in to a better player.\n\n\"Ray Reardon was that guy and my dad made that phone call and within 24 hours of making a phone call, Ray was up in Sheffield, we met and from then on we had this unbelievable relationship.\n\n\"And I always say, I need to be shown what to do on a snooker table. And once I'm shown what to do I can only incorporate that into my game.\n\n\"The word was 'I'm going to make you into an impregnable player' and I didn't even know what 'impregnable' meant. The popular Welshman, nicknamed \"Dracula\" because of his distinctive widow's peak hairstyle, died on Friday night after a battle with cancer, his wife Carol has confirmed.\n\nA former coal miner and police officer, Reardon was at the forefront of the game's rise to popularity as a televised sport during the 1970s and is credited with influencing the next generation of superstars.\n\nCompatriot and three-time world champion Mark Williams told the World Snooker Tour's official website: \"Ray is one of the best sports people ever from Wales and the best snooker player. He's one of the reasons why a lot of us started playing.\n\nRead more:Three North Wales chip shops named on list of best 23 in the UK\n\nRead more:Bizarre patterns on remote North Wales hill leave people baffled amid raft of conspiracy theories\n\n\"He put snooker on the map, alongside Alex Higgins, Jimmy White and Steve Davis. Anyone playing now owes them a lot because they brought popularity to the game. He is a real inspiration.\"\n\nWhite paid his own tribute in a post on X which read: \"Gutted to hear my very good friend Ray Reardon has passed away. A total class act and very kind to me when I was making my way in the game. A giant of the game. Rest in Peace, mate.\"\n\nBorn in Tredegar in 1932, Reardon took up snooker and billiards as a youngster and, having followed his father down the pit at Ty Trist Colliery, proved so adept at the former that he won the Welsh Amateur Championship every year from 1950 to 1955.\n\nHe swapped the mines for a career with the police but at the age of 35, and after beating John Spencer to lift the English Amateur title in 1964, he turned professional.\n\nReardon's first world championship campaign ended in a 25-24 quarter-final defeat by Fred Davis in 1969, but he won the title for the first time a year later and repeated the feat in 1973, 1974, 1975, 1976 and 1978, the last of his wins coming at the Crucible after the tournament's move to the Sheffield venue 12 months earlier.\n\nThat win over South Africa's Perrie Mans established the then 45-year-old as the oldest man to claim the world crown, a record surpassed by Ronnie O'Sullivan in 2022.\n\nReardon reached one more world final in 1982, when he was beaten by Higgins, but won 16 other professional tournaments during a career which saw him crowned as the sport's first number one when rankings were introduced in 1975.\n\nHe retired in 1991, but continued to play into his nineties and compiled a century break in November last year.\n\nReardon's death prompted an outpouring of affection for a man whose warmth and humour helped to make him a household name.\n\nDennis Taylor, winner of the 1985 world championship, said on X: \"So sad to hear of the passing of the great Ray Reardon. Would always be in my top 8 of all time.\n\n\"Had the pleasure of traveling around the world with a true gentleman. Condolences to all his family. RIP Ray.\"\n\nShaun Murphy, world champion in 2005, said: \"What a man you were Ray. An absolute animal on the table, and a total gentleman off it. It was an honour to spend time with you whether we played snooker or golf - I'll treasure every minute. Rest in peace.\"\n\nFormer player and current TV presenter Neal Foulds posted: \"Awfully sad news that Ray Reardon has passed away. A giant of our sport. Thanks for the memories Ray. {"uri": "8234920297", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "11:49:47", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T11:49:47Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T11:48:57Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7960784435272217, "url": "https://jerseyeveningpost.com/uksport/2024/07/20/ray-reardon-the-world-beating-cueman-who-swapped-coal-mine-for-crucible/", "title": "Ray Reardon: The world-beating cueman who swapped coal mine for Crucible", "body": "Ray Reardon ruled snooker in the 1970s when the man known fondly as 'Dracula' became a household name.\n\nYet his story almost ended long before the Welshman with the widow's peak found stardom.\n\nOn April 30, 1957, the world collapsed around the ears of the 24-year-old Reardon as a mining accident almost claimed his life.\n\nHe was fortunate to see the next day, let alone land six World Championship titles - his last in 1978 aged 45.\n\nReardon, who has died at the age of 91, was born on October 8, 1932 in Tredegar.\n\nHe left Georgetown Secondary School at 14 and, after a brief stint as an apprentice motor mechanic, he followed his father Ben down the pits, firstly at nearby Ty Trist and later at the Pochin Colliery.\n\nThe teenage Reardon displayed an aptitude for snooker and was spared evening shifts to allow him to practise. Unlike in the modern game, however, there was little money to be made from potting balls.\n\nCutting coal underground was the career choice, and when Welsh mines began to close the Reardon family uprooted to North Staffordshire. Ben and Ray Reardon found work at the Florence Colliery in 1956 and in the following year disaster almost struck.\n\nThe future world-beating cueman was developing a pit roadway when a \"trickle of dirt\" fell on his helmet. Sensing danger, he hurled himself down and when a 12-foot girder crashed down along with a deluge of rubble, Reardon was trapped.\n\nHe was rescued after three hours, Reardon drawing on memories of playing marbles with brother Ron - 17 years his junior - to avoid panicking.\n\nIn his 1982 autobiography, Reardon recalled: \"My ordeal had left me looking and feeling a bit like a dirty dishcloth and when the blood started to circulate in my numb legs it had me screaming in pain for a moment or so, but they gave me sips of water and soon I was all right.\"\n\nDuring war time, mother Cynthia had bought the young Ray a 4ft x 2ft billiard table for Christmas and he exhibited natural talent.\n\nOne afternoon, after attending a headmaster's funeral, Reardon ducked out of a school line and into the Tredegar Workmen's Institute Society, where he won a frame against venue manager Tommy Biggs, who had a wooden leg. Reardon was swiftly collared by his maths teacher.\n\nBy the age of 13 Reardon was representing the Institute's men's team in the Sirhowy Valley Billiards and Snooker League. He was soon champion of all Wales.\n\nAs a miner, Reardon wore white gloves similar to those of a snooker referee, but after meeting future wife Sue his career moved above ground.\n\nRay and Sue married on Easter Monday in 1959 and Reardon joined the Stoke-on-Trent police force a year later, staying for almost eight years.\n\nHe won the English amateur championship in 1964, beating John Spencer, and a year later became a father when Sue gave birth to their son Darren. Daughter Melanie followed in 1969.\n\nReardon considered staying with the police, but snooker won out. The BBC television show Pot Black was a hit, even while many watched in black and white, and Reardon defeated Spencer to win the first edition in 1969.\n\nHe lost his opener at the World Championship in the same year 25-24 to the 55-year-old Fred Davis. But a year later, the 37-year-old Reardon ruled the snooker world for the first time, beating John Pulman 37-33 in the final. He said that \"nothing will ever surpass the wonder\" of the moment.\n\nReardon's first car was a Ford Prefect he labelled the 'Gutless Wonder'. Later, after upgrading, he owned the 1 PRO licence plate.\n\nManchester staged the 1974 World Championship and, come the closing session of the final against Graham Miles, Reardon introduced the outfit that would forever associate him with Bram Stoker's blood-sucking Count Dracula.\n\nStoke-based Marsden's Tailors sponsored Reardon and he emerged attired in cape, top hat and scarlet silk jacket.\n\n\"Someone decided the outfit made me look like Dracula and that name stuck to me more than most,\" Reardon later said.\n\nReardon for many years played exhibitions in South Africa during the apartheid era, explaining the trips by saying: \"The only world I was trying to change was the world of snooker.\"\n\nTwelve months later and Reardon reigned at the Crucible when a 25-18 victory over Perrie Mans clinched a sixth world title at the age of 45 years and 203 days.\n\nFour years later he returned to the final but was beaten 18-15 by Alex Higgins.\n\nReardon remained competitive until the mid-1980s and retired in 1991.\n\nHis marriage to Sue ended in divorce during the twilight years of his playing career.\n\nReardon made Brixham, and later Torquay, his home, and spent many happy years as president of Churston Golf Club.\n\nIn 2004 he teamed up with Ronnie O'Sullivan in a mentoring role at the World Championship and helped to steer the Rocket to his second world title. He had received a call from O'Sullivan's imprisoned father, Ronnie snr, to encourage the union.\n\nReardon, who is survived by his second wife Carol, saw the Welsh Open trophy named in his honour in 2016 but savoured his quiet retirement in Devon, saying: \"It's nice on the golf course.\"", "source": {"uri": "jerseyeveningpost.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "Jersey Evening Post"}, "authors": The Welshman passed away on Friday night, having previously been diagnosed with cancer.\n\n\"Ray is one of the best sports people ever from Wales and the best snooker player,\" said the former world champion, Mark Williams. \"He's one of the reasons why a lot of us started playing. Reardon, endearingly known as 'the Gentleman', was a dominating force in the 1970s snooker scene, with six World Championship titles under his belt, reports the Daily Star and Birmingham Live.\n\nGet the latest news straight to your phone by joining us on WhatsApp\n\nHe made history as the first snooker player to hold the World No. 1 rank when the system debuted in 1976. In a chat with Torbay Today last year, the Welsh Snookering icon expressed disbelief in an interview with Torbay Today. He said: \"Absolutely, a 100 break, couldn't believe it. I always wanted to beat my age... got a couple of 70s some time back and was happy with that.\n\n\"Then suddenly I did it ... 100. Could not believe it! There cannot be many, if any, people in their 90s that have had a 100 break, I must be the only one in the world. Sadly, there is nobody left from my snooker circuit who is still playing, come to think of it.\"\n\nReacting to the news of Ray's death, fellow snooker star Jimmy White shared his sadness via Twitter, writing: \"Gutted to hear my very good friend Ray Reardon has passed away. A total class act and very kind to me when I was making my way in the game. A giant of the game. Rest in Peace mate.\"\n\nDuring his sporting career, Reardon was fondly referred to as Dracula due to his unique widow's peak and pointed smile. Up until his passing, he was often spotted with a golf club as much as a snooker cue, holding the position of president at Churston Golf Club in Brixham, Devon. Ben and Ray Reardon found work at the Florence Colliery in 1956 and in the following year disaster almost struck.\n\nThe future world-beating cueman was developing a pit roadway when a \"trickle of dirt\" fell on his helmet. Sensing danger, he hurled himself down and when a 12-foot girder crashed down along with a deluge of rubble, Reardon was trapped.\n\nHe was rescued after three hours, Reardon drawing on memories of playing marbles with brother Ron - 17 years his junior - to avoid panicking.\n\nReardon won English amateur championship in 1964, beating John Spencer, and in 1969 won the first edition of the hit television show Pot Black.\n\nHe lost his opener at the world championship in the same year to 55-year-old Fred Davis. But a year later, the 37-year-old Reardon ruled the snooker world for the first time, beating John Pulman in the final.\n\nManchester staged the 1974 World Championship and, come the closing session of the final against Graham Miles, Reardon introduced the outfit that would forever associate him with Bram Stoker's blood-sucking Count Dracula.\n\nStoke-based Marsden's Tailors sponsored Reardon and he emerged attired in cape, top hat and scarlet silk jacket.\n\n\"Someone decided the outfit made me look like Dracula and that name stuck to me more than most,\" Reardon later said.\n\nHe won four straight world titles from 1973 to 1976. Then, as defending champion, Reardon had the honour of playing on the first morning when the tournament moved to Sheffield's Crucible Theatre in 1977. He beat Patsy Fagan before falling to eventual champion Spencer.\n\nTwelve months later and Reardon reigned at the Crucible when victory over Perrie Mans clinched a sixth world title at the age of 45.\n\nReardon remained competitive until the mid-1980s and retired in 1991.", "source": {"uri": "au.sports.yahoo.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "Yahoo!7 Sport"}, "authors": [], "concepts":
