Rest in Peace, mate.\"\n\nBorn in Tredegar in 1932, Reardon took up snooker and billiards as a youngster and, having followed his father down the pit at Ty Trist Colliery, proved so adept at the former that he won the Welsh Amateur Championship every year from 1950 to 1955.\n\nHe swapped the mines for a career with the police but at the age of 35, and after beating John Spencer to lift the English Amateur title in 1964, he turned professional.\n\nReardon's first world championship campaign ended in a 25-24 quarter-final defeat by Fred Davis in 1969, but he won the title for the first time a year later and repeated the feat in 1973, 1974, 1975, 1976 and 1978, the last of his wins coming at the Crucible after the tournament's move to the Sheffield venue 12 months earlier.\n\nThat win over South Africa's Perrie Mans established the then 45-year-old as the oldest man to claim the world crown, a record surpassed by Ronnie O'Sullivan in 2022.\n\nReardon reached one more world final in 1982, when he was beaten by Higgins, but won 16 other professional tournaments during a career which saw him crowned as the sport's first number one when rankings were introduced in 1975.\n\nHe retired in 1991, but continued to play into his nineties and compiled a century break in November last year.\n\nReardon's death prompted an outpouring of affection for a man whose warmth and humour helped to make him a household name.\n\nShaun Murphy, world champion in 2005, said on X: \"What a man you were Ray. An absolute animal on the table, and a total gentleman off it. It was an honour to spend time with you whether we played snooker or golf - I'll treasure every minute. {"uri": "8235164279", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "15:47:40", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T15:47:40Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T15:46:44Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7058823704719543, "url": "https://www.mirror.co.uk/sport/other-sports/snooker/ray-reardon-ronnie-osullivan-snooker-33288050", "title": "Ray Reardon claimed 'son' Ronnie O'Sullivan 'put 10 years on' his life", "body": "Ronnie O'Sullivan had such a good relationship with Ray Reardon that the Welshman once said his former protege, who he thought of as a son, added 10 years to his life\n\nLate snooker legend Ray Reardon once claimed Ronnie O'Sullivan was like a son to him.\n\nThe Welshman, who passed away on Friday at the age of 91, used to mentor The Rocket and is credited with pushing his game to another level. Under Reardon's tutelage, O'Sullivan won his second world title and was World No. 1 for two years straight.\n\nSpeaking about their historic partnership last year, Reardon said the soon-to-be seven-time world champion \"put 10 years on my life\". He told the BBC: \"When we got to know each other, it was like father and son, really. It was wonderful, fantastic.\"\n\nTheir pairing began in 2004, when Reardon, who earned the nickname 'Dracula' thanks to his widow's peak and prominent teeth, received a call from O'Sullivan's father, who was in prison at the time.\n\nDuring the call, O'Sullivan Sr. convinced Reardon, himself a six-time world champion, to lend a hand to his son, who had been underperforming for several years. When they met, Reardon gave O'Sullivan a big hug and told him he'd make him \"impregnable\".\n\n\"I remember him saying, 'You pot balls for fun, no one's better than you but we're going to teach you how to become impregnable',\" O'Sullivan recalled. \"I was like, 'What does that mean?' And he went, 'Well, no weaknesses'. We're going to work on your defensive game.' And that was it really. We just kind of got to work.\"\n\nReardon immediately recognised O'Sullivan's quality, and said he remembers thinking, \"This is the best player I've ever seen\". A few months later O'Sullivan won the world title and celebrated by wearing a set of Dracula fangs, in honour of his mentor, during the trophy ceremony.\n\n\"Ray's come down here and he's added a new dimension to my game,\" he said after the triumph. \"And I don't want to give no secrets away but he's really improved me in certain areas and I think that's where I've needed to develop as a player.\"\n\nWith Reardon's teachings embedded in his brain and skilled hands, O'Sullivan went on to win five further world titles, in 2008, 2012, 2013, 2020 and 2022. His victory in the latter saw him become the oldest world champion in snooker history at 46, breaking the record a 45-year-old Reardon had set back in 1978.\n\nSpeaking at the start of 2023, ahead of the Masters Snooker event, the Wordsley-born star explained the enduring legacy of his former coach. \"Ray Reardon brought a side to the game I never knew existed,\" O'Sullivan told Eurosport. \"I learnt it and now I appreciate it and use it a lot, and when I have to.\"\n\nReardon turned professional in 1967 after a short career as a police officer. He won his first world title in 1970 and won five more before the end of the decade. He was also the first player to be ranked World No. 1 when the rankings were introduced in 1976.\n\nHe eventually retired in 1991 at the age of 59, six years after receiving an MBE for his services to snooker.\n\nJoin our new WhatsApp community and receive your daily dose of Mirror Football content. champion Ray Reardon has died after a battle against cancer at the age of 91.\n\nThe Welshman dominated the sport in the 1970s, claiming six world titles between 1970 and 1978.\n\nNicknamed 'Dracula' because of his distinctive widow's peak hairstyle, Reardon became a star as the game enjoyed a television boom.\n\nHe won the first Pot Black series on BBC Two in 1969, and was appointed MBE in the 1985 Queen's Birthday honours.\n\nHis rivalries with John Spencer and the flamboyant Alex \"Hurricane\" Higgins made for some of the most compelling sporting contests on television at the time.\n\nReardon's first world title came in 1970 when he beat John Pulman by 39 frames to 34.\n\nThere followed a gap until 1973 when he beat Australian Eddie Charlton in the final, the first of four consecutive titles, culminating in his victory over Higgins in 1976 - the last Championship before the move to The Crucible in Sheffield.\n\nReardon won the title again in 1978, and topped the world rankings until 1981 when the emergence of Steve Davis heralded a new era.\n\nThe former coal miner and a police officer reached the World Championship final again in 1982, but lost to Alex Higgins.\n\nReardon retired from the professional game in 1991 at the age of 58.\n\nHe was later hired by Ronnie O'Sullivan as a consultant and was credited with helping the talented Londoner claim {"uri": "8235088393", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "14:30:38", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T14:30:38Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T14:29:53Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.5686274766921997, "url": "https://www.stokesentinel.co.uk/news/stoke-on-trent-news/incredible-story-how-ray-reardon-9425630", "title": "How Ray Reardon survived Potteries colliery disaster and became snooker legend", "body": "The name Ray Reardon means a lot to sports fans in Stoke-on-Trent. He may have been born in Wales but when he moved to the Potteries he truly became one of our own.\n\nIt's no surprise that tributes have been flooding in today after the news of Ray's death at the age of 91 emerged. We have taken this piece from the archives to again tell the story of how Ray went from a miner at Florence Colliery, a police officer in Hanley to becoming one of the greatest snooker players the nation has ever seen.\n\nThis is the story about Ray's remarkable life published back in 2001:\n\n\"The former England and Yorkshire cricketer, Freddie Trueman, used to say that if the England team needed a good, reliable fast bowler years ago the selectors had to do one thing. That was to stand at the opening of a mineshaft and whistle. Without doubt a strapping miner with the ability to bowl quickly would appear out of the darkness.\n\n\"Ray Reardon was a miner who came from the Welsh Valleys. His hometown was Tredegar and it was in that town that his love for the game of snooker developed. Even to the point where he would nip off from school to go down to the snooker hall to attempt to increase his pocket money. It was no surprise that as a 12-year-old he was playing in the Welsh boys' snooker championship.\n\n\"Ray left school at 14 and hadn't reached the age of 15 when he started working in the pit. Although he was working at the colliery he continued to practise his snooker. He became amateur champion of Wales when he was 18.\n\n\"When you look at Ray's Roll of Honour it makes impressive reading. He was Amateur Champion of Wales from 1950 until 1955.\n\n\"A year later, the Reardon family moved to North Staffordshire. Pit closures forced his father, Ben, to make the move. He was taken on at Florence Colliery and Ray joined him there.\n\n\"One day, a 12ft-long heavy girder came crashing down on a boulder close to Ray. Fortunately a gap appeared and he was able to breathe. It took 12 men to get the rubble off him. Three hours later he was freed. Bruised, battered but no further the worse for wear. What a lucky escape!\n\n\"Ray's mind moved to other areas of employment and he applied and was accepted into the police force in Stoke-on-Trent. He was placed in Hanley and in 1960, P.C. Ray Reardon No 184 was nicking criminals, not heaving coal.\n\n\"His snooker was continuing well and the Police force encouraged his participation, which was in his own time. He was the Great Britain Police Champion four times between 1962 and 1966 and the English Amateur Champion in 1964.\n\n\"I had no ambition to be a professional,'' admitted Ray. \"It was something that hadn't crossed my mind until I went to South Africa in 1967.'' He went there with John Barron and took part in a Test series against South Africa which produced a 2-1 win.\n\n\"Ray did turn professional and won the first of his six World Championships in 1970. He defeated John Pulman of Bromley in a 73-frame final at the Victoria Hall, London. Ray became Stoke-on-Trent's first World Champion in any sport. \"\n\nMore on Ray's incredible in achievements in snooker can be found in our tribute article here.\n\nSign up to our main daily {"uri": "2024-07-428004302", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "11:09:07", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T11:09:07Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T11:00:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.5568627715110779, "url": "https://www.delgazette.com/2024/07/20/liberty-twp-fire-chief-details-need-for-more-funding/", "title": "Liberty Twp. fire chief details need for more funding", "body": "Liberty Township Fire Chief James Reardon went before Powell City Council on Tuesday to detail the department's growing need for a future levy to support its increasingly expensive operations.\n\n\"I've been the fire chief for just over a year-and-a-half, and there are four main topics that keep popping up on my radar: staying competitive, increased costs, the ability to plan, and then, unfortunately, we're at a point of need at this point,\" Reardon told the council to begin his presentation. \"What I mean by staying competitive is we're here to provide a service; we don't provide a commodity. That service requires people to do that job, and the quality of people you have determines the level of service you provide. Today's market is not about just finding the right people but how do you keep them?\"\n\nReardon noted there are a lot of \"lateral transfers\" happening in the Columbus area, meaning people who have spent years with an agency are now leaving for other agencies for \"a few more dollars or different benefits.\"\n\n\"The problem is when people leave, they're taking with them an incredible amount of knowledge, and the money that our agency has invested in them leaves as well,\" he added.\n\nAccording to Reardon, the department invested an average of $160,000 per firefighter in 2023 when accounting for salary, benefits, training, education and uniforms. He pointed out there are people in the department with more than 30 years of tenure, multiplying the investment made in the individual significantly while also amplifying the loss of resources if they leave.\n\nSpeaking on the increased cost of operations, Reardon said the cost of fire gear for a single firefighter has increased 81% since 2020. New fire trucks now cost approximately $1.2 million, an 89% increase since 2019, and ambulances cost an average of $450,000, which represents a 133% increase since 2021. The average maintenance cost of vehicles has grown to $172,000, a 274% increase since 2020.\n\nAs for the department's ability to plan, Reardon said calls for service have increased by 38% since 2020, and the number of overlapping calls increased from 22% to 49% in 2023.\n\n\"Every other run, when one ambulance leaves, the other is leaving, too. That leaves no one available for the next one,\" Reardon said.\n\nReardon added the department takes a number of calls determined not to be emergencies, and he suggested the community would benefit from a \"community paramedic\" who can respond to those calls rather than dedicating a firetruck or ambulance to those calls.\n\nUltimately, Reardon said the department has done very well to remain frugal through the years, but the cost to provide the services expected in the community is arriving at a level where \"something has to give.\"\n\n\"Since 2020, we've seen a 16% increase in population, a 36% increase in operational cost, and a 38% increase in calls for service,\" Reardon said to close his presentation. \"As costs continue to rise, our revenue does not (rise). The fire and EMS tax accounts for 4.7% of the property taxes collected in the city of Powell. ... Overall, our costs have increased 67% in the life of this levy, which is 10 years. Eventually, the amount collected is not enough to pay for the inflationary cost of operations. We've maintained our current income for 10 years, and unfortunately, our primary source of funding does not include an inflationary mechanism. This is why we ask the board to go the voters for additional money this fall.\"\n\nReach Dillon Davis at 740-413-0904.
